  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1fc8e5c4-074a-44c2-bc74-0d4d62c6902d'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

  <span id="freeText14741497513381710596" style="display:none"><b>Superb Stories, Daring Deeds, Fantastic Adventures</b><br /><br />Here is the action-packed sequel to <i>Boy</i>, a tale of Dahl's exploits as a World War II pilot.     <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2186257667" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2186257667" />
    <a title="Miranda Reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads"><img alt="Miranda Reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592243041p2/71848701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2186257667?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miranda Reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miranda Reads" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads">Miranda Reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780224024075" href="/book/show/1423397.Going_Solo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=2018-popsugar-reading-challenge">2018-popsugar-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2186257667" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3710350820851082233"><b>Whew! This man did more within a year than I have in my life.</b><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i> And the giraffes would incline their heads very slightly and stare down at me with languorous demure expressions, but they never ran away. I found it exhilarating to be able to walk freely among such huge graceful wild creatures and talk to them as I wished.</i>
</blockquote><br/>This is the second half of Dahl's autobiography. <b>
  <i>What a wild life! </i>
</b>Right after high school, Roald packed up his belongings, got a job for the Shell company and set off for </span>
  <span id="freeText3710350820851082233" style="display:none"><b>Whew! This man did more within a year than I have in my life.</b><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i> And the giraffes would incline their heads very slightly and stare down at me with languorous demure expressions, but they never ran away. I found it exhilarating to be able to walk freely among such huge graceful wild creatures and talk to them as I wished.</i>
</blockquote><br />This is the second half of Dahl's autobiography. <b>
  <i>What a wild life! </i>
</b>Right after high school, Roald packed up his belongings, got a job for the Shell company and set off for Africa. <b>For three years.</b> No visits home, no calling his mom, just up and leaves his beloved family for <b>a life of adventure.</b> And during his time in Africa, a lion carries away a woman, a black mamba attacks his servant and a green mamba invades a house to kill a family dog. <br /><br />It's quite interesting to hear <b>an unfiltered account</b> of that time. For example, all the men at the Shell company had a "boy." Now, this "boy" was actually<b> a full grown man.</b> He had a wife (sometimes wives) to support and essentially acted as a butler. He spoke Swahili and so did Roald (it was not considered right to force the "boys" to learn English). Roald taught his boy how to read and write, and his boy tended Roald's every need. It was strange to read about.<br /><br />Only, before Roald could finish his time with the Shell company...<b>the Great War broke out.</b> And <i>that</i> was quite a story in itself. Roald joined the airforce and was trained as a pilot. As in, he was give <b>7 and a half hours</b> of in-flight training before being declared fit for service along with fifteen other new pilots. Then, they were given fighter planes and told to get up in the air. Unsurprisingly, this happened:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>It is a fact, and I verified it carefully later, that out of those sixteen, no less than thirteen were killed in the air within the next two years</i>
</blockquote><br />All this before he turned twenty-five. The things he had to live through churned my stomach - e specially the account of how he crashed his first plane. Dahl spares no details and, as always, <b>his stories were absolutely fascinating.</b><br /><br /><i>The PopSugar 2018 Reading Challenge - A childhood classic you never read</i><br /><br /><a href="http://bit.ly/33ii2CT" rel="nofollow noopener">YouTube</a> | <a href="http://www.mirandareads.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/miranda.reads/?hl=en" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/Miranda_Reads" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | Snapchat @miranda.reads <br /><br />Happy Reading!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3710350820851082233" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5208051261265423741">Wow, Roald was in some series air battles during World War II. I mean he could have easily died. It might not be true, but I think he came through all that mess so he could write these children's stories for the world. He should have died many times during the Battle for Athens and all the other times he went up in the air. <br/><br/>There was a time he was talking about flying 300 miles and hour so low to the ground to escape being shot down that he had to lift up to not hit cows and walls on the ground.</span>
  <span id="freeText5208051261265423741" style="display:none">Wow, Roald was in some series air battles during World War II. I mean he could have easily died. It might not be true, but I think he came through all that mess so he could write these children's stories for the world. He should have died many times during the Battle for Athens and all the other times he went up in the air. <br /><br />There was a time he was talking about flying 300 miles and hour so low to the ground to escape being shot down that he had to lift up to not hit cows and walls on the ground. He did have a plane crash. He spent 3 years in Africa and Greece. <br /><br />I love the first part of this story when he is on a steamer heading to Africa and he tells of all the exotic passengers on the trip. Expats love doing dotty things and they did some dotty things. It was a great character study. The book starts out with the colonel and his wife running on the deck of the ship totally naked. No wonder he is so great with characters. He lived a really interesting life. <br /><br />I don't feel like I have done enough after listening to him, but my life is my life. Like he says, we don't travel the same way we used to. Flying somewhere is not the same as a boat trip that stops at many ports. He did some crazy things in Africa as well. He was 6'6". He was huge. <br /><br />I had fun reading this and I was totally into his adventures. I also appreciate that he never took pleasure in killing people. He was always relieved to see people's parachutes open if he shot them down. I appreciate him and his works even more now. He is a hero of mine. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1870786931" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1870786931" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5903843-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603562140p2/5903843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1870786931?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/5903843-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5903843-matt?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1870786931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16669317914958474698">“A life is made up of a great number of small incidents and a small number of great ones.” So opens the second and 'adult-based' portion of Roald Dahl's autobiography. He makes perfectly clear that this is not such a book, for autobiographies are full of useless and boring information. Dahl seeks to offer the reader some of the key memories he had during his early adult life, particularly serving in the Second World War. Accepting a job with the Shell Company, Dahl is soon shipped to the African</span>
  <span id="freeText16669317914958474698" style="display:none">“A life is made up of a great number of small incidents and a small number of great ones.” So opens the second and 'adult-based' portion of Roald Dahl's autobiography. He makes perfectly clear that this is not such a book, for autobiographies are full of useless and boring information. Dahl seeks to offer the reader some of the key memories he had during his early adult life, particularly serving in the Second World War. Accepting a job with the Shell Company, Dahl is soon shipped to the African continent, working particularly in Dar es Salaam, part of what is currently Tanzania. During his travel aboard a ship to reach the far shores, Dahl learns why the upper class held use of hands when eating in such low regard and the 'daytime entertainment' they found acceptable on deck. Arriving in Africa, Dahl uses all his patience and understanding as he undertook a complete culture shock, a world where wildlife ran the show and humans knew their place. While the scenery was spectacular and the people highly entertaining, the rumblings of war could be heard on the horizon. Dahl finds himself evacuated from the region, only to join the RAF to help Britain in the forthcoming Second World War. Lanky, yet determined, Dahl is an unlikely pilot-in-training before he became a key member of the effort in North Africa. While serving, Dahl is involved in a significant aerial accident, captured in a story he penned, eventually bastardised and published in the Saturday Evening Post. Dahl seeks to correct the narrative for the reader in this piece of writing, as if one might worry he was seeking to make himself seem overly heroic. The crash leaves Dahl with significant injury, his nose caved into his fractured skull (interesting for those who remember his childhood injury to the same nose), and he is required to remain in hospital for upwards of two months. While he convalesces, Dahl continues penning his weekly letters to his mother, though remains careful to censor his news, so as not to have the letters destroyed. Once healthy enough to fly again, Dahl heads out to serve in Greece, where he comes face to face with the Nazis, trying to hold the onslaught back and keep the Allies in control of the area. As the fighting intensified, Dahl dodges many a proverbial bullet and heads to the Middle East, where he sobers up to much of what was going on in the region and the European Theatre, learning of the extreme anti-Semitism or ignored undertone of the Nazi atrocities. As the reader is pulled deeper into the life of this wonderful author, Dahl uses his wonderful prose to breathe life into his life story. A must read for anyone who loves a story of humour and utter despair, all in short order.<br /><br />Having recently completed the first volume in this first-person narrative, I wanted to take some time to explore the adult life of this man whose stories tantalised me throughout my childhood. As Dahl continues the story, boarding schools are replaced with African-style boardrooms (open air villages) and a collection of characters that only Dahl could dream up. Those Dahl mentions turned from being men recollected into individuals with complex backstories or who shaped Dahl in his intense battles in Africa and the war zones he discovered. Using a collection of his letters penned to his mother, Dahl is able to recall some of the minutiae, which helps substantiate his many adventures. Dahl outwardly admits the need to use this letters, both because of his age when writing the tale and the number of events that can mesh together in wartime. Crisp and humorous, Dahl is able to tell his story while keeping the reader spellbound, injecting passion for his situations on every page while not getting too wordy in his descriptions. A contrast to stories about chocolate-makers, giants, and fresh produce, this story (and its first volume) is not one to be passed over. It is yet another gem in the Roald Dahl collection.<br /><br />Kudos, Mr. Dahl for this two-volume collection about your life prior to becoming the popular writer for which you are best remembered. I cannot wait to tackle some of your other works, adult- and child-centric alike.<br /><br />Like/hate the review? An ever-growing collection of others appears at: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://pecheyponderings.wordpress.com/">http://pecheyponderings.wordpress.com/</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16669317914958474698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_3099246" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3099246" />
    <a title="Miund" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/189144-miund"><img alt="Miund" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442471735p2/189144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3099246?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miund" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miund" href="/user/show/189144-miund">Miund</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3099246" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10070017201221287113">First I read 'Boy' and then I read 'Going Solo'. This book is perfect. Roald Dahl tells the grown-up part of his life in such a way that made me crave more for his adventures in the Royal Air Force. It's like sitting in a cafe, listening to your date who's telling you amusing stories of his life. He's a hundred years older than you are, yet you still find him attractive! </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3567233892" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3567233892" />
    <a title="Settare" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13198822-settare"><img alt="Settare" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590610257p2/13198822.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3567233892?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Settare" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Settare" href="/user/show/13198822-settare">Settare</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141322742" href="/book/show/2752490-going-solo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13198822-settare?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13198822-settare?shelf=childhood-favorites">childhood-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13198822-settare?shelf=biography-memoir">biography-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13198822-settare?shelf=british-irish">british-irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13198822-settare?shelf=read-many-times">read-many-times</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13198822-settare?shelf=wwii">wwii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13198822-settare?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3567233892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2906077860725676311">I have a very personal relationship with <i>Going Solo</i> (one of my favorite books by one of my all-time favorite authors), so this review is going to be a bit personal and perhaps somewhat irrelevant. <br/><br/><b><i>My</i> Going Solo</b><br/>As a child, I'd devoured all of Dahl's books including this one, but its depth and underlying horror hadn't really registered with me as a nine-year-old. <br/>Then, a decade later when I was nineteen, I was about to go on my very first 'grand' solo adventure: a backpacking trip to South Africa </span>
  <span id="freeText2906077860725676311" style="display:none">I have a very personal relationship with <i>Going Solo</i> (one of my favorite books by one of my all-time favorite authors), so this review is going to be a bit personal and perhaps somewhat irrelevant. <br /><br /><b><i>My</i> Going Solo</b><br />As a child, I'd devoured all of Dahl's books including this one, but its depth and underlying horror hadn't really registered with me as a nine-year-old. <br />Then, a decade later when I was nineteen, I was about to go on my very first 'grand' solo adventure: a backpacking trip to South Africa to stay and work in a wildlife conservation reserve (I was an ecstatic first-year Zoology student with big dreams about saving all the Cheetahs and Rhinos). Like many other travelers before an upcoming trip into the unknown, I got cold feet the night of departure, I almost wanted to cancel the whole thing and I was in desperate need of a story to distract me from my fear and encourage me to go on. So, one minute before leaving for the airport I snatched <i>Going Solo</i> from the shelf to take with me. I didn't recall the details, but I knew it was about a somewhat similar adventure, a solo trip to Africa, anyway. It saved me from anxiety and kept me hooked, awed, and encouraged through the airport fiasco and two long-winded flights. I took it to the wildlife reserve and read Dahl's descriptions of giraffe and elephants as I looked at the same animals in the distance. I formed an intimate bond with the book on that trip. So, <i>Going Solo</i> became my companion on all my solo trips after that. Whenever I am somewhere far out of my comfort zone and starting to wish I could run back to the comfort of my own bed, I take out this book, read my favorite highlights and forget I was scared of anything at all. I have cherished this book as a personal companion and motivational text, but that's only a tiny part of what this book is about and why it's so valuable.<br /><br /><b>Summary of The Book</b><br />Going Solo picks up where Dahl's childhood biography, <i>Boy</i> left off. In 1938, the 22-year-old Dahl signs a three-year contract to work in East Africa for three years with Shell. At this point, he's a typical young Englishman of his era: the world is his to discover and conquer, and the British Empire is there to help him in his great adventures and discoveries (he does not bat an eyelid about colonialism, of course). In the first part of the book, Dahl tells the stories of his tripe to Dar-es-Salaam on board a ship (so many funny parts), his stay in Tanzania which is filled with encounters with Mamba snakes, lions (and a "casually funny" story of a lion abducting a woman), giraffes, elephants, "dotty" colonialist Englishmen, and the local people, most notably his local valet Mdisho. This part of the book is more light-hearted than the rest and is filled with Dahl's typical deft sense of humor, his never-ending curiosity, wonder, and attentive observations: <br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I often amazed myself by the way I behaved when I was certain that there were no other human beings within fifty miles. All my inhibitions would disappear and I would shout, ‘Hello, giraffes! Hello! Hello! Hello! How are you today?’ And the giraffes would incline their heads very slightly and stare down at me with languorous demure expressions, but they never ran away. I found it exhilarating to be able to walk freely among such huge graceful wild creatures and talk to them as I wished.”</i>
</blockquote><br />Then, in 1939 the war breaks out and the events start to get more and more harrowing. On the days he receives the official news of the start of the war, the 23-year-old Dahl is stationed on the road to Mozambique and is ordered to stop German civilians to get out of the country. His orders are to open fire on them if they resist. (it doesn't come to that, fortunately, but one man is killed).<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“I was twenty-three and I had not yet been trained to kill anyone. I wasn’t absolutely sure that I could bring myself to give the order to open fire on a bunch of German civilians in cold blood should the necessity arise. I was feeling altogether very uncomfortable in my skin. [...]<br />What was I going to do, I asked myself, if they refused to go back and tried to barge their way through? I knew there and then that I could never quite bring myself to give the order for the machine-gun to mow them all down. It would be an appalling massacre. I stood there and said nothing.”<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />After that, he decides to join the airforce and goes on to flight training in Kenya and Iraq. Before he officially starts his service, he suffers a serious injury and nearly escapes death in a crash and is hospitalized for several months. He goes back and joins battle and flies fighter airplanes in Greece, Libya, and Palestine, miraculously surives many near-death situations and is finally forced to go back to England in 1941, because his head injuries leave him incapable to fly. <br />His experience shows how mindless the battle strategies were, how the priority of the commanders was symbolic action at any cost instead of the lives of soldiers, how, instead of evacuating on time and saving many pilots they were ordered to stay and "protect" a fleet that didn't even exist, and how many colossal losses of human life could have been prevented if pilots with minimal training weren't sent on perilous missions on their first flights, and generally if the commanders weren't so incapable and mindless.<br /><br /><b>Reflections</b><br />What I summed up in a few lines actually occupies most of the book and is some of the most terrifying, most haunting, most comic, and most light-hearted war memoirs I've ever read. Only Roald Dahl can write about war, airplanes crashing, oil tankers being bombed, people burned alive and lives lost, and STILL fit charm and humor in it.<br /><blockquote><i>“Dear Mama,<br />We’ve been doing some pretty intensive flying just lately – you may have heard about it a little on the wireless. [...] We’ve lost 4 pilots killed in the Squadron in the last 2 weeks, shot down by the French. Otherwise this country is great fun and definitely flowing with milk and honey …”</i><br />- Excerpt from one of the letters to his mother, many of which are included in the book.</blockquote><br />His writing really demonstrates how ridiculous, how pointless, how grotesque war is at its core. Much of the world he lived in is absolutely alien and distant to me. I will never understand a world without long-distance telephone calls, I can't understand what it's like to be 23 and having to decide whether to shoot down 7o civilians of the "enemy", (at least I <i>hope</i> I won't have to do that in the next 16 months until I reach 24, but I think it's a safe bet that it won't come to that), I can't understand what it's like to be on air surrounded by 200 enemy aircraft ready to shoot you down, but I can relate to the humor, the deeply human feelings, the pain and joy and the spirit of adventure; and to the allure of "going solo". I am so grateful that Roald Dahl survived those atrocities and came to write most of my favorite childhood books and this memoir. But Dahl is only <i>one</i> person who managed to miraculously get out of <i>one</i> war alive, and he went on to become a terrific writer. I can't help but wonder, with a sting of longing, what about all the other potentially fantastic writers, artists, scientists, and generally life and joy that <i>didn't</i> survive all the wars humans have fought throughout history? What if they hadn't perished? What if all that potential wasn't lost for virtually nothing? I don't know. Surely, I would've wanted to read all of their works and memoirs as well. <br /><br />Anyway, this book is one of my favorites of all time, partly because of the personal sentimental value that I've found in it over the years. It's been my trusty companion for whenever I'm <i>Going Solo</i> or getting lonely. <br />I recently listened to the audiobook narrated brilliantly by Dan Stevens (highly, highly recommended) on an early morning solitary run. While listening to the funniest parts, I was laughing out loud from behind my mask as I was jogging, which probably made a ridiculous sight and I'm sure a number of early morning commuters who saw me assumed I was insane. That's fine. I'm okay with looking silly when I'm immersed in Roald Dahl's world. It's a fantastic escape every single time. Everyone in his books is out of their minds anyway, and I love it.<br /><br /><br /><b>Other works that remind me of this book:</b> (Recommended if you enjoyed Going Solo)<br />- Hayao Miyazaki's anime films <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0104652/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Porco Rosso</i>
</a> and <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt2013293/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Wind Rises</i>
</a> <br />- Antoine de Saint-Exupéry's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/229806.Pilote_de_guerre" title="Pilote de guerre by Antoine de Saint-Exupéry" rel="nofollow noopener">Pilote de guerre</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2906077860725676311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/300964269?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Irina Elena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Irina Elena" href="/user/show/7664770-irina-elena">Irina Elena</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3599296167?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter Tillman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter Tillman" href="/user/show/8101737-peter-tillman">Peter Tillman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141303109" href="/book/show/474038.Going_Solo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8101737-peter-tillman?shelf=bios-memoirs">bios-memoirs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8101737-peter-tillman?shelf=travel">travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8101737-peter-tillman?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3599296167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15238251425592229715">Re-read after many years. What I liked most this time were his recollections of his shipboard trip out from England to Africa, with eccentric "Empire Builder" Englishmen returning to their colonial jobs. Dahl had been hired by Shell Oil to be a company rep in Tanganyika, visiting clients and taking orders for fuel and lubricants. For me, the memorable stories were about wildlife: a huge lion snatched up a native cook's wife, carried her off in his mouth, then released her unharmed. A deadly gree</span>
  <span id="freeText15238251425592229715" style="display:none">Re-read after many years. What I liked most this time were his recollections of his shipboard trip out from England to Africa, with eccentric "Empire Builder" Englishmen returning to their colonial jobs. Dahl had been hired by Shell Oil to be a company rep in Tanganyika, visiting clients and taking orders for fuel and lubricants. For me, the memorable stories were about wildlife: a huge lion snatched up a native cook's wife, carried her off in his mouth, then released her unharmed. A deadly green mamba went into an Englishman's house. He called the local snake-catcher, another eccentric Englishman, who caught the snake. Sadly, the family dog had already been killed. The sheer abundance of wildlife in those days (the late 1930s).<br /><br />His flying lessons and time as a fighter pilot, in the doomed and ill-advised British effort to defend Greece. Dahl learned on the job and survived. Most of his comrades-in-arms didn't. The pitifully small British air detachment was largely destroyed. <br /><br />His writing is wry, vivid and really good. I'm glad to have read the book again. I think he caught the flavor of those times and places really well. Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15238251425592229715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2382205978" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2382205978" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202724-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601676054p2/202724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2382205978?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/202724-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141326320" href="/book/show/7137670-going-solo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202724-jessica?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2382205978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2737593415545429404">My WORD. This was an eye-opening combination of humorous "Englishman abroad" and hair-raising WWII memoir! Starting out with his time, just out of school, as a Shell Oil employee in Dar es Salaam, the story moves right into the outbreak of WWII and Dahl's becoming an RAF pilot, because that is exactly what happened. He was never able to go home, and for three years his only contact with his beloved mother and sisters were the letters that they managed to send each other. Dahl's trademark humor a</span>
  <span id="freeText2737593415545429404" style="display:none">My WORD. This was an eye-opening combination of humorous "Englishman abroad" and hair-raising WWII memoir! Starting out with his time, just out of school, as a Shell Oil employee in Dar es Salaam, the story moves right into the outbreak of WWII and Dahl's becoming an RAF pilot, because that is exactly what happened. He was never able to go home, and for three years his only contact with his beloved mother and sisters were the letters that they managed to send each other. Dahl's trademark humor and complete lack of respect for authority make for an interesting look at war in northern Africa, to say the least!<br /><br />But seriously, though: I don't think God intended Roald Dahl to have a nose. First there's the car accident in Boy. Then there's the plane crash in this one. I'm amazed he looked as good as he did as an older man!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2737593415545429404" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1684654971" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1684654971" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1684654971?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1684654971" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer82297307010258553">'Going Solo' is the second instalment of Dahl's autobiography. Whilst interesting, informative and entertaining this may be - it isn't anything like as compelling as the first book covering Dahl's earlier years - 'Boy'. Still definitely worth reading - for all fans of Dahl and anyone with an interest in his pre-literary life. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1902674317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5944930511907018393">This is the second autobiography of Roald Dahl's life. The first book was Boy: Tales of Childhood. I suggest reading them in order, but it wouldn't be the worst thing ever if you didn't! <br/>Here is my review on the first book: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1664300785?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br/><br/>This book starts at the age of 18 (and the first book goes up to the age 18). I really enjoyed this book! BUT, it only focused on him fighting in the war. I was hoping it would cover more than that. The war only lasted for a couple of y</span>
  <span id="freeText5944930511907018393" style="display:none">This is the second autobiography of Roald Dahl's life. The first book was Boy: Tales of Childhood. I suggest reading them in order, but it wouldn't be the worst thing ever if you didn't! <br />Here is my review on the first book: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1664300785?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><br />This book starts at the age of 18 (and the first book goes up to the age 18). I really enjoyed this book! BUT, it only focused on him fighting in the war. I was hoping it would cover more than that. The war only lasted for a couple of years... He did get badly hurt and almost die, so that was extremely interesting to read about. It was crazy to think what literature would be like if Roald had died in the war.<br /><br />However, this book left me with a ton of unanswered questions. Like, what happened when he went home after the war? What did he do for work? How did he start writing, etc? I felt it was only an autobiography about a tiny bit of his life. It was probably the most tragic part of his life, but still only a little bit of it. It made me feel like there was still a lot missing or that there should have been another book after Going Solo. <br /><br />I suggest this book to anyone that enjoys him as a an author or likes autobiographies.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6816675475725334195">3.5 stars. <br/><br/>First off, I am once again shocked that this is considered juvenile fiction. I could see this being for the older range of juvenile fiction (15-18), but I would NOT give this to my little siblings (ages 12-14). There was quite a bit of language, which made me uncomfortable. Yeah, I get it; it's real life, but nonetheless. <br/><br/>Anyway, besides that, I really enjoyed this book! It took me 5 or so chapters to really be interested in it, but after that I was hooked. Dahl's slightly humerous an</span>
  <span id="freeText6816675475725334195" style="display:none">3.5 stars. <br /><br />First off, I am once again shocked that this is considered juvenile fiction. I could see this being for the older range of juvenile fiction (15-18), but I would NOT give this to my little siblings (ages 12-14). There was quite a bit of language, which made me uncomfortable. Yeah, I get it; it's real life, but nonetheless. <br /><br />Anyway, besides that, I really enjoyed this book! It took me 5 or so chapters to really be interested in it, but after that I was hooked. Dahl's slightly humerous and fascinating accounts of a RAF pilot were so interesting. It also portrayed a side of WWII that you don't hear a lot about: the fighting in the Middle East, which was were Dahl was stationed. <br /><br />Overall, a good read, but would not recommend to anyone under the age of 15 due to the aforementioned langauge, and somewhat mild description of violence. </span>

  <span id="freeText11921010937228003656" style="display:none">A sequel to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2506560976" rel="nofollow noopener">Boy</a>, this picks up with Dahl’s journey to East Africa to take up a position with Shell. <div id="review_384221168" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/384221168" />
    <a title="Andy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1251066-andy"><img alt="Andy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593516272p2/1251066.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/384221168?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy" href="/user/show/1251066-andy">Andy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140325287" href="/book/show/1423399.Going_Solo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1251066-andy?shelf=illustrated">illustrated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1251066-andy?shelf=autobiography">autobiography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1251066-andy?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer384221168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8133183788300357195">Following on from Boy, Going Solo was another tremendously important book to me as a child. Where I could relate to his boyhood tales in some way, the next part of his life was a complete window to another world. Read then it was extraordinary and magical; read now I appreciate it on different levels entirely.<br/><br/>Dahl mentions how lucky he felt to have witnessed the later days of colonial Britain and the people that made the empire. All negative issues relating to Colonialism aside (I'm not going to</span>
  <span id="freeText8133183788300357195" style="display:none">Following on from Boy, Going Solo was another tremendously important book to me as a child. Where I could relate to his boyhood tales in some way, the next part of his life was a complete window to another world. Read then it was extraordinary and magical; read now I appreciate it on different levels entirely.<br /><br />Dahl mentions how lucky he felt to have witnessed the later days of colonial Britain and the people that made the empire. All negative issues relating to Colonialism aside (I'm not going to go there and neither does Dahl) I completely understand what he means. The first half detailing his time in Africa working for the Shell Company is brilliant; a window to a life that no longer exists, with long boat journeys, quirky slightly mad Englishmen (and ladies) abroad, the culture and way of life. It's clearly romanticised; a big adventure, but then it's portrayed with such vigour and love that you can see the appeal to a fresh faced early-20 year old. I would have loved it (and probably still would). As a boy it made me dream of African countryside, baking suns, lions, deadly snakes and a different world.<br /><br />The second part, detailing his experiences in the war as a pilot is equally enthralling though very different in tone. This was my first exposure to the second world war in any real way (back at primary school in the late-80s - we didn't cover the wars until secondary school and my subsequent interest developed a few years after that) and Dahl makes it all seem jolly exciting. Almost over before he began, his initial adoration of flying is powerfully detailed before his (more truthfully documented) account of the crash that put him in hospital for 6 months. <br /><br />After, we have a series of raids and dogfights which become somewhat mechanical and repetitive in nature but still hold the interest. I wonder now though, whether his emphasis on a jolly adventure isn't quite as truthful as it could be; he states a few times that looking back he wonders why he wasn't more scared. I wonder the same. The horrors of war only really peak through at times and I suspect this was him writing for a younger audience.<br /><br />Put together though, both Boy and Going Solo are wonderful books for children to open their eyes to different types of stories and worlds. Dahl's relaxed narrative envisages a cosy fire and glass of whiskey, reminiscent of an afternoon with your granddad. Equally of interest to the adults too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8133183788300357195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2661766116" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2661766116" />
    <a title="Liz V." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5929789-liz-v"><img alt="Liz V." src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2661766116?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz V." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz V." href="/user/show/5929789-liz-v">Liz V.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142413838" href="/book/show/4702041-going-solo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2661766116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8984675942680432634">In 1938, Dahl embarks on a ship to his new job in Africa, experiencing his first, eye opening encounters with British civil servants. Once established in his job, having successfully mastered sufficient Swahili, Dahl travels extensively with vivid descriptions of elephants, giraffes, lions and snakes--big, bad, deadly snakes. Nonetheless, Dahl is having the time of his life, although everyone knows war is coming. <br/><br/>The balance of the book recounts Dahl's enlistment in the RAF, the pitiful training</span>
  <span id="freeText8984675942680432634" style="display:none">In 1938, Dahl embarks on a ship to his new job in Africa, experiencing his first, eye opening encounters with British civil servants. Once established in his job, having successfully mastered sufficient Swahili, Dahl travels extensively with vivid descriptions of elephants, giraffes, lions and snakes--big, bad, deadly snakes. Nonetheless, Dahl is having the time of his life, although everyone knows war is coming. <br /><br />The balance of the book recounts Dahl's enlistment in the RAF, the pitiful training on antiquated equipment, and experiences with ill prepared leaders. One of the latter sends him off with the wrong coordinates, resulting in a crash landing in no-man's land and months in hospital. <br /><br />Recovered, Dahl is sent to Greece, fighting a rearguard action as the Germans pour soldiers and planes into the country that their Italian allies failed to secure. Dahl clearly disagrees with the decision, which I found odd given his scathing comments on Vichy Frenchmen in Syria. As was apparent in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1313856.Fortress_Malta_An_Island_Under_Siege_1940_43" title="Fortress Malta An Island Under Siege 1940-43 by James Holland" rel="nofollow noopener">Fortress Malta: An Island Under Siege 1940-43</a>, the British high command was halfhearted in implementing Churchill's policy, as well as ensuring dissemination of the hard learned lessons of the Battle of Britain to fliers in other theaters of war. <br /><br />The book ends with Dahl's return to England, repatriated due to recurring headaches from his head injuries back in Egypt. <br /><br />Throughout, Dahl comes across as a genuinely nice man, from the nightly hour spent teaching his servant to read and write English and Swahili through his struggles with the necessity of killing the enemy in war. <br /><br />My knowledge of Dahl had been limited to a few anecdotes about his unhappy, and shortlived, time as Assistant Air Attache in hard partying D.C., his more successful time with the British Security Coordination, and his marriage to Patricia Neal. I intend to backtrack, to read about his younger years in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2485611.Boy_Tales_of_Childhood" title="Boy Tales of Childhood by Roald Dahl" rel="nofollow noopener">Boy: Tales of Childhood</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8984675942680432634" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1058576151" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1058576151" />
    <a title="Emrys Elkouh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24265588-emrys-elkouh"><img alt="Emrys Elkouh" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1058576151?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emrys Elkouh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emrys Elkouh" href="/user/show/24265588-emrys-elkouh">Emrys Elkouh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1058576151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14363189752989812778">In English class, we read the autobiography <i>Going Solo</i> by Roald Dahl. The book is all about his adventures in Africa while working for the Shell Oil Company, and while flying for the Royal Air Force in World War Two. The book also contains some of Dahl’s encounters with animals. He wrote it to tell the reader what it was like to live and explore in the African Savanna. <br/><br/>This book had some strong themes. The language can take a while to decipher, and can be quite annoying at times. At other times,</span>
  <span id="freeText14363189752989812778" style="display:none">In English class, we read the autobiography <i>Going Solo</i> by Roald Dahl. The book is all about his adventures in Africa while working for the Shell Oil Company, and while flying for the Royal Air Force in World War Two. The book also contains some of Dahl’s encounters with animals. He wrote it to tell the reader what it was like to live and explore in the African Savanna. <br /><br />This book had some strong themes. The language can take a while to decipher, and can be quite annoying at times. At other times, it is easier to get the meaning out of a phrase. Dahl calmly states, “‘I think that we’re going to get killed’” (<i>GS</i> pg. 154), to his friend David Coke in the middle of the war. This shows that his view on life has changed throughout his experiences in war. Some of the weaknesses in Dahl’s writing were when he made it seem like there would be a great description on what happened, and sometimes there was none. That was very disappointing. I would probably recommend this book to others if they have an interest in adventure, and true stories. <br /><br />Dahl’s writing style really helped me understand what was happening most of the time. He writes in a very easy to understand type of writing. I like his style, and would read all of his books if given the chance. The plot also helped with his style. I am very interested in WWII, and that is what half of this book was about. This book has also given me a different view on autobiographies. I do not have any connections to the story, but I feel that I will develop some in the future, as I age and have more experiences. <br /><br />I will end on my favorite quote from the book. It is, “But there is a world of difference between falling in love with a voice and remaining in love with a person you can see.” (<i>GS</i> pg. 115) This quote has a lot of meaning behind it, and that is why I like it. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14363189752989812778" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4993021495551564307"><i>From BBC Radio 4 - Drama:<br/>To celebrate the centenary year of his birth, a full dramatization of Roald Dahl's gripping autobiographical overseas adventure.<br/><br/>Beginning aboard the SS Mantola, Dahl sets sail for Africa at the tender age of 22. He experiences the remnants of colonial British life, filled with eccentric characters, and is thrown into a world as bizarre and surprising as any you will find in his fiction.<br/><br/>"Life is made up of a great number of small incidents and a small number of great ones</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4993021495551564307" style="display:none"><i>From BBC Radio 4 - Drama:<br />To celebrate the centenary year of his birth, a full dramatization of Roald Dahl's gripping autobiographical overseas adventure.<br /><br />Beginning aboard the SS Mantola, Dahl sets sail for Africa at the tender age of 22. He experiences the remnants of colonial British life, filled with eccentric characters, and is thrown into a world as bizarre and surprising as any you will find in his fiction.<br /><br />"Life is made up of a great number of small incidents and a small number of great ones."<br /><br />1/2: Stationed in Tanzania, Dahl is faced with the excitement of the wild; lions carrying off women in their mouths; fatal green mambas captured by snake men. But his savannah-sun-drenched life is interrupted when World War II erupts. Dahl is ordered to round up the German inhabitants of Dar es Salaam and experiences first-hand the horror of war.<br /><br />2/2: As World War II rages, Pilot Officer Dahl takes to the air in a series of daring deeds. An inspirational account of survival when things seem hopeless, in which the extraordinary is made human.<br /><br />"The second part is about the time I spent flying for the RAF in the Second World War. There was no need to discard anything from this period because every moment was, to me at least, completely enthralling."<br /><br />Having joined the RAF Dahl discovers a love of flying. But a crash in the Western desert almost ends his war before he's started. Eventually he rejoins his heavily depleted squadron during the hopeless last days in Greece. Dogged air fights, secret missions and many narrow misses with death ensue before he eventually returns home to his loving mother.<br /><br />Patrick Malahide provides the voice of Dahl in a colourful adaptation by Lucy Catherine.<br /><br />Dramatised by Lucy Catherine<br /><br />Directed by Helen Perry<br />A BBC Cymru/Wales Production.</i><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b07hwwjv">http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b07hwwjv</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15782977258603039624">The book Going Solo by <i>Roald Dahl</i> was a real experience of what happened to him during World War II. Before WWII he went to Africa to work for the Shell Oil Company. During WWII he became a pilot in England. Roald Dahl wrote 48 books in his life.   <span id="freeText15782977258603039624" style="display:none">The book Going Solo by <i>Roald Dahl</i> was a real experience of what happened to him during World War II. Before WWII he went to Africa to work for the Shell Oil Company. During WWII he became a pilot in England. Roald Dahl wrote 48 books in his life. This book is interesting and I would recommend it. <br /><br />The strength of this book was its excitement. Dahl seemed crazy he talked to animals. One time he talks to the giraffes, “Hello, giraffes! Hello! Hello! Hello! How are you today?” (<i>Going Solo p79</i>). The weakness of the book was that it was difficult to understand because of the unique British spelling and vocabulary. Dahl used several themes, including beauty and danger. <br /><br /> Bwana, it is a beautiful sword. With one blow it cut through his neck so deeply that his whole head fell forward and dangled down on to his chest, and as he started to topple over I gave the neck one more quick chop and the head came right away from the body and fell to the ground like a coconut (<i>G.S p72</i>). <br /><br />I liked this book because I learned a lot of things about WWII. I would recommend this book to young adults because Dahl used some words that were difficult to understand. Or if you want to learn about Dahl’s life and WW2, it would be a good choice to read.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13532167581729374955">I wish I could say I have been all over the world, have crashed landed a plane, or have shot-down German aces, which is why I really admire Roald Dahl. Dahl leaves England at age twenty one to work for the Shell oil company in Africa. Dahl worked in Africa until the outbreak of World War II, when he enlisted in the RAF and learned to fly warplanes.   <span id="freeText13532167581729374955" style="display:none">I wish I could say I have been all over the world, have crashed landed a plane, or have shot-down German aces, which is why I really admire Roald Dahl. Dahl leaves England at age twenty one to work for the Shell oil company in Africa. Dahl worked in Africa until the outbreak of World War II, when he enlisted in the RAF and learned to fly warplanes. After becoming a pilot officer, Dahl fought in Greece as it was overtaken by the Germans. Dahl’s book “Going Solo” tells all about his adventures with vivid detail. He describes lion attacks and dog fights with a classic British style that portrays the attitudes of the characters he interacted with so well that you feel as though you new them. After reading the book, I felt like I had met ex-patriots from India, RAF veterans in the midst of battle, African natives chasing lions, and Jewish refugees dreaming of their homeland. While reading, I still laughed out loud during most pages at his characteristically British wit that is almost tongue-in cheek. Perhaps more than any other feeling the reader gets a sense of awe for the adventures of he and his generation, along with an inspiration to go on grand escapades yourself. </span>

<div id="review_199392581" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="KOMET" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4183137-komet"><img alt="KOMET" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598176739p2/4183137.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/199392581?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="KOMET" class="user" itemprop="url" name="KOMET" href="/user/show/4183137-komet">KOMET</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140103069" href="/book/show/461224.Going_Solo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4183137-komet?shelf=biographies-diaries-and-memoirs">biographies-diaries-and-memoirs</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer199392581" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14316358672378380314">Many years ago, I read this book, which thoroughly captivated me. Before earning a worldwide reputation as a writer of exciting and wildly imaginative children’s books, Dahl had served as a fighter pilot in the Royal Air Force during the Second World War. Here he relates his experiences of undergoing flight training in Southern Africa. He conveys with touching clarity the stresses, joys, and pain any pilot trainee experiences in coming to grips with flying. As someone who is fascinated with avia</span>
  <span id="freeText14316358672378380314" style="display:none">Many years ago, I read this book, which thoroughly captivated me. Before earning a worldwide reputation as a writer of exciting and wildly imaginative children’s books, Dahl had served as a fighter pilot in the Royal Air Force during the Second World War. Here he relates his experiences of undergoing flight training in Southern Africa. He conveys with touching clarity the stresses, joys, and pain any pilot trainee experiences in coming to grips with flying. As someone who is fascinated with aviation, I almost felt as if I were in the cockpit with Dahl as he advanced through the various levels of training. <br /><br />Dahl went on to serve with a fighter squadron in Libya, which, in the wake of the German blitzkrieg in the Balkans in April 1941, was later sent to Greece. It proved to be a fruitless undertaking as Dahl’s squadron sustained heavy losses and Dahl himself was greviously wounded. This book is a testament to one man’s love for flying and his efforts to pick up the pieces and resume a full life again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14316358672378380314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2964653628" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2964653628" />
    <a title="Vartika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63883678-vartika"><img alt="Vartika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601807242p2/63883678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2964653628?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vartika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vartika" href="/user/show/63883678-vartika">Vartika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141322742" href="/book/show/2752490-going-solo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2964653628" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6558074439787015700">I have Roald Dahl to thank for a huge chunk of my love for reading. Because <i> Matilda </i> was the first full-length book I ever read (cover-to-cover and over and over at that); I have often been tempted to buy more of his works way out into adulthood, for their charm is simply unmatchable. It seemed imperative to me one of these days as I passed down Book Street in Mumbai that I have to read Dahl again as a 21 year old, and so I grabbed a copy of <i> Going Solo </i> and got right to it. <br/><br/><i> Going Solo </i> is par</span>
  <span id="freeText6558074439787015700" style="display:none">I have Roald Dahl to thank for a huge chunk of my love for reading. Because <i> Matilda </i> was the first full-length book I ever read (cover-to-cover and over and over at that); I have often been tempted to buy more of his works way out into adulthood, for their charm is simply unmatchable. It seemed imperative to me one of these days as I passed down Book Street in Mumbai that I have to read Dahl again as a 21 year old, and so I grabbed a copy of <i> Going Solo </i> and got right to it. <br /><br /><i> Going Solo </i> is part two of the author's autobiography, and deals with his experiences during the second World War, first as an employee of the Shell Oil Company in Dar-es-Salaam, and then as a fighter pilot in the RAF. While narrated with the author's characteristic sense of observation and of humour, this book nevertheless is a much deeper reflection on a harrowing military experience, and of the ravages and wastes of the World Wars in general. <br /><br />At first, Dahl sets out to Africa to work for Shell, and — after a curious ship journey where he meets a lot of 'dotty' englishmen serving in the British Empire — records his adventurous encounters with deadly Mamba snakes, lions who mysteriously abduct women; and also his friendship with his 'boy', Mdisho. <br />One can see in the beginning, that as <i> bwana </i> of a small Tanzanian hamlet, 21-year-old Dahl has a certain sense of civilizational authority and affectionate patronizing as a white Englishman, one who has left home for three years in order to see the world and perhaps help govern it. <br /><br />However, as the war breaks out, Dahl — in an act of patriotism and daring, but also as means of exploring the skies — enlists himself in the Royal Air Force. Here begins the retelling of horrors and harrowing experienced that not only amaze one for having taken place at all, but also reveal the negligence in certain aspects of the war as sheer, mindless power-play which led to the death of many pilots and military men. In the words of the author and his colleague, David Coke, it [the war and particularly the British mission in the Middle East] was a complete 'cock-up'. <br /><br />Dahl's experiences as a Pilot Officer, in fact, act as a great exposé of military operations of the war, where action, however mindless, was more important than the lives of soldiers and young men. Dahl himself is sent on his very first combat mission after very minimal training on antiquated equipment and <i> no experience whatsoever </i> of manning the kind of 'kite' assigned to him. His own brushes with death; and his accounts of the depersonalized deaths of others on his squadron; make known that a massive number of the deaths in the war were a result of the negligence and lack of training imparted by the British, rather than martyrdom caused by direct enemy action. <br /><br />The propagandist nature of war is revealed in the story of Dahl's crash landing in no-mans land, which was a result of being provided incorrect co-ordinates by ill-advised leaders, and led to long months immobilised in the hospital. This near-death experience, he says, was modified by an American Magazine to be result of enemy action in the skies, rather than what it was: the absolute unpreparedness of the British forces for this war. <br /><br />In Greece, Dahl's Squadron 80 is composed of about 15 fighter planes against several hundreds of German bombers, fighters and other deadly aircrafts that spewed fire, and is entrusted with preventing a German takeover of the country as well as supposedly protecting the Navy as it leaves the shores of Greece (except that it is never to be found). Unbelievable, isn't it? Dahl is one of the only 7 pilots left alive at the end of a few weeks, and escapes death by a hair's breadth in several incidents including the Battle of Athens. Without proper ground crew, equipment, and (in Megara, Greece) even food and water, the squadron is ordered to keep functioning and 'following orders' that nobody is sure of. The myopic idea of suffering in war is seen in Dahl recounting his meeting some Jewish Refugees in Palestine with no idea of what was happening to them. In the end, Dahl is repatriated due to splitting headaches that resulted from his grave injuries in Egypt, and goes home to a war-ravaged England. <br /><br />Dahl wrote <i> Going Solo </i> over forty years after the war ended. However, his striking recollection of events and his own reactions to them makes one remember the kinds of damage done to people by war, in both life and death. Dahl personalises his experiences even more by including in the book his (unanswered) letters home to his mother; excerpts from his official Log Book and telegrams sent by the authorities; and pictures taken by him through these years on his Zeiss camera. <br /><br />While the book is listed as reading for Young Adults and can introduce them to the nuances of war, it is an incredibly reflective and insightful account of war for all ages. Indeed, the Quentin Blake-illustrated cover is misleading. <br /><br />Roald Dahl's life and experiences was worth way more than just the fiction he wrote, and <i> Going Solo </i> makes that evident as never before.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6558074439787015700" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16436782921288615382">This (These) books surprised me like not many have ever before.<br/>.<br/>#theguywiththebookreview presents Boy And Going Solo by Roald Dahl.<br/>.<br/>🍩 - Yes none of these are fake, they were sent to me by @timhortonssaudi 🥳<br/>.<br/>Both Boy and Going Solo are Dahls Autobiography’s, he didn’t call them autobiography’s and said they are only recollections of his strongest memories, memories that have had a lasting enough affect to still be remembered!<br/>.<br/>Boy very much reads like his fictional children’s books and there’s joy</span>
  <span id="freeText16436782921288615382" style="display:none">This (These) books surprised me like not many have ever before.<br />.<br />#theguywiththebookreview presents Boy And Going Solo by Roald Dahl.<br />.<br />🍩 - Yes none of these are fake, they were sent to me by @timhortonssaudi 🥳<br />.<br />Both Boy and Going Solo are Dahls Autobiography’s, he didn’t call them autobiography’s and said they are only recollections of his strongest memories, memories that have had a lasting enough affect to still be remembered!<br />.<br />Boy very much reads like his fictional children’s books and there’s joy, laughter and hope through out! But there is a lot of pain in it too. He studied in a boarding school and teachers didn’t think twice before physically punishing children. Both books are filled with snippets of letters to his mom (He absolutely adored his mother and was her only son) and there was one revelation in Going Solo that made me so so emotional. I’d recommend you pick just because of that.<br />.<br />His first time sailing out of UK and His unplanned adventures across Africa where he witnesses Lions attacking people and learns a new language. His training to become a fighter pilot (Yes!! He flew and successfully dropped quite a lot of Nazi planes too!)<br />.<br />Once you’re done reading both these books, you’ll realize that Roald Dahl’s greatest achievements were never related to the written word.<br />.<br />Did I tell you he was from Norway and was 6’6” tall! 😳</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1psPX2W1H7z+RXfnCDajLD0kn6MKerC+ZF8RTgkMKWl+BpRll/VS+VpgLRL3anoyst8Jv+eqiXPwxCAkVeFjRw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('f/ZXjdk4wgFEN+/hUcmbGiLDWwngyZbizB2HwQHnxUXXa8y3K3iPROAStRSulUIErTjNFQ0Zry9YhrarXQqPaw==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11500525554985850643">Roald Dahl was a British novelist, short story writer and screenwriter of Norwegian descent, who rose to prominence in the 1940's with works for both children and adults, and became one of the world's bestselling authors.<br/><br/>Dahl's first published work, inspired by a meeting with C. S. Forester, was <i>Shot Down Over Libya</i>. Today the story is published as <i>A Piece of Cake</i>. The story, about his wartime adv</span>
  <span id="freeText11500525554985850643" style="display:none">Roald Dahl was a British novelist, short story writer and screenwriter of Norwegian descent, who rose to prominence in the 1940's with works for both children and adults, and became one of the world's bestselling authors.<br /><br />Dahl's first published work, inspired by a meeting with C. S. Forester, was <i>Shot Down Over Libya</i>. Today the story is published as <i>A Piece of Cake</i>. The story, about his wartime adventures, was bought by the Saturday Evening Post for $900, and propelled him into a career as a writer. Its title was inspired by a highly inaccurate and sensationalized article about the crash that blinded him, which claimed he had been shot down instead of simply having to land because of low fuel.<br /><br />His first children's book was <i>The Gremlins</i>, about mischievous little creatures that were part of RAF folklore. The book was commissioned by Walt Disney for a film that was never made, and published in 1943. Dahl went on to create some of the best-loved children's stories of the 20th century, such as <i>Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</i>, <i>Matilda</i> and <i>James and the Giant Peach</i>.<br /><br />He also had a successful parallel career as the writer of macabre adult short stories, usually with a dark sense of humour and a surprise ending. Many were originally written for American magazines such as <i>Ladies Home Journal</i>, <i>Harper's</i>, <i>Playboy</i> and <i>The New Yorker</i>, then subsequently collected by Dahl into anthologies, gaining world-wide acclaim. Dahl wrote more than 60 short stories and they have appeared in numerous collections, some only being published in book form after his death. His stories also brought him three Edgar Awards: in 1954, for the collection <i>Someone Like You</i>; in 1959, for the story "The Landlady"; and in 1980, for the episode of <i>Tales of the Unexpected</i> based on "Skin".</span>
  <a data-text-id="11500525554985850643" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
